      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        LRO
       </b>
       ) is a NASA
       <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">
        robotic spacecraft
       </a>
       currently orbiting the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       in an eccentric polar mapping orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petro2014_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Petro2014-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whereislro_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-whereislro-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Data collected by LRO has been described as essential for planning NASA's future human and robotic missions to the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20090416_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa20090416-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its detailed mapping program is identifying safe landing sites, locating potential resources on the Moon, characterizing the radiation environment, and demonstrating new technologies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-overview-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mission_design_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mission_design-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Launched on June 18, 2009,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-launch_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-launch-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in conjunction with the
       <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">
        Lunar Crater Observation and Sensing Satellite
       </a>
       (LCROSS), as the vanguard of NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Precursor_Robotic_Program" title="Lunar Precursor Robotic Program">
        Lunar Precursor Robotic Program
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       LRO was the first United States mission to the Moon in over ten years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcia_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marcia-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       LRO and LCROSS were launched as part of the United States's
       <a href="/wiki/Vision_for_Space_Exploration" title="Vision for Space Exploration">
        Vision for Space Exploration
       </a>
       program.
      </p>
      <p>
       The probe has made a 3-D map of the Moon's surface at 100-meter resolution and 98.2% coverage (excluding polar areas in deep shadow),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20111118_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-space20111118-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including 0.5-meter resolution images of Apollo landing sites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceNASA_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ScienceNASA-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LROC_Apollo_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LROC_Apollo-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first images from LRO were published on July 2, 2009, showing a region in the lunar highlands south of
       <a href="/wiki/Mare_Nubium" title="Mare Nubium">
        Mare Nubium
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Sea of Clouds
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The total cost of the mission is reported as US$583 million, of which $504 million pertains to the main LRO probe and $79 million to the LCROSS satellite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNET_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CNET-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>
      <div class="toc" id="toc">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mission">
        Mission
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_V(401)_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="1996" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Atlas_V%28401%29_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg/140px-Atlas_V%28401%29_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Atlas_V%28401%29_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg/210px-Atlas_V%28401%29_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Atlas_V%28401%29_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg/280px-Atlas_V%28401%29_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg 2x" width="140"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_V(401)_launches_with_LRO_and_LCROSS.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">
          Atlas V
         </a>
         carrying LRO and LCROSS
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Developed at NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">
        Goddard Space Flight Center
       </a>
       , LRO is a large (1,916 kg/4,224 lb
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNET_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CNET-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) and sophisticated spacecraft. Its mission duration was planned for one year,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extension2_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-extension2-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but has since been extended numerous times after review by NASA.
      </p>
      <p>
       After completing a preliminary design review in February 2006 and a critical design review in November 2006,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the LRO was shipped from Goddard to
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">
        Cape Canaveral Air Force Station
       </a>
       on February 11, 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Launch was planned for October 2008, but this slid to April as the spacecraft underwent testing in a thermal vacuum chamber.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Launch was rescheduled for June 17, 2009, because of the delay in a priority military launch,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and happened one day later, on June 18. The one-day delay was to allow the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour">
        Space Shuttle Endeavour
       </a>
       a chance to lift off for
       <a href="/wiki/STS-127" title="STS-127">
        mission STS-127
       </a>
       following a hydrogen fuel leak that canceled an earlier planned launch.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Areas of investigation include
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/selenodetic" title="wikt:selenodetic">
        selenodetic
       </a>
       global
       <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">
        topography
       </a>
       ; the lunar
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">
        polar regions
       </a>
       , including possible
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_water" title="Lunar water">
        water ice
       </a>
       deposits and the lighting environment; characterization of deep space
       <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">
        radiation
       </a>
       in lunar orbit; and high-resolution mapping, at a maximum resolution of 50 cm/pixel (20 in/pixel), to assist in the selection and characterization of future landing sites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klotz_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Klotz-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition, LRO has provided images and precise locations of landers and equipment from previous American and Russian lunar missions, including the Apollo sites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceNASA_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ScienceNASA-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Payload">
        Payload
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Payload">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LRO_payload.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="487" data-file-width="500" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/LRO_payload.jpg/220px-LRO_payload.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/LRO_payload.jpg/330px-LRO_payload.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/LRO_payload.jpg/440px-LRO_payload.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LRO_payload.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Onboard instruments
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The orbiter carries a complement of six instruments and one technology demonstration:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cosmic_Ray_Telescope_for_the_Effects_of_Radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cosmic Ray Telescope for the Effects of Radiation (page does not exist)">
         CRaTER
        </a>
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The primary goal of the Cosmic Ray Telescope for the Effects of Radiation is to characterize the global lunar radiation environment and its biological impacts.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
         <a href="#cite_note-27">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
       <dt>
        <a href="/wiki/Diviner" title="Diviner">
         Diviner
        </a>
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The Diviner Lunar Radiometer Experiment measures lunar surface thermal emission to provide information for future surface operations and exploration.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
         <a href="#cite_note-28">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
       <dt>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lyman-Alpha_Mapping_Project&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lyman-Alpha Mapping Project (page does not exist)">
         LAMP
        </a>
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The Lyman-Alpha Mapping Project peers into permanently shadowed craters in search of water ice, using
        <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
         ultraviolet
        </a>
        light generated by stars as well as the hydrogen atoms that are thinly spread throughout the
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
         Solar System
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
         <a href="#cite_note-29">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
       <dt>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Exploration_Neutron_Detector&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lunar Exploration Neutron Detector (page does not exist)">
         LEND
        </a>
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The Lunar Exploration Neutron Detector provides measurements, creates maps, and detects possible near-surface water ice deposits.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
       <dt>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbiter_Laser_Altimeter" title="Lunar Orbiter Laser Altimeter">
         LOLA
        </a>
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The Lunar Orbiter Laser Altimeter investigation provides a precise global lunar topographic model and geodetic grid.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       <span id="LROC">
       </span>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter_Camera" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter Camera">
         LROC
        </a>
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter Camera addresses the measurement requirements of landing site certification and polar illumination.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
         <a href="#cite_note-31">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
        LROC comprises a pair of narrow-angle
        <a href="/wiki/Push_broom_scanner" title="Push broom scanner">
         push-broom imaging cameras
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
         <a href="#cite_note-32">
          [32]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
         <a href="#cite_note-33">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (NAC) and a single wide-angle camera (WAC). LROC has flown several times over the historic
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">
         Apollo
        </a>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_landing" title="Lunar landing">
         lunar landing
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_missions" title="List of Apollo missions">
         sites
        </a>
        at 50 km (31 mi) altitude; with the camera's high resolution, the
        <a href="/wiki/Lunar_rover" title="Lunar rover">
         lunar rovers
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
         Lunar Module
        </a>
        descent stages and their respective shadows are clearly visible, along with other equipment previously
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_on_the_Moon" title="List of artificial objects on the Moon">
         left on the Moon
        </a>
        . The mission is returning approximately
        <span class="nowrap">
         70–100
         <a href="/wiki/Terabyte" title="Terabyte">
          terabytes
         </a>
        </span>
        of image data. It is expected that this photography will boost public acknowledgement of the validity of the landings, and further discredit
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Moon_Landing_hoax_theories" title="Apollo Moon Landing hoax theories">
         Apollo conspiracy theories
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceNASA_15-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-ScienceNASA-15">
          [15]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
       <dt>
        <a href="/wiki/Mini-RF" title="Mini-RF">
         Mini-RF
        </a>
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The Miniature Radio Frequency radar demonstrated new lightweight
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_aperture_radar" title="Synthetic aperture radar">
         SAR
        </a>
        and communications technologies and located potential water-ice.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
         <a href="#cite_note-34">
          [34]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Names_to_the_Moon">
        Names to the Moon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Names to the Moon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Prior to the LRO's launch,
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       gave members of the public the opportunity to have their names placed in a microchip on the LRO. The deadline for this opportunity was July 31, 2008.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huntsville_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Huntsville-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       About 1.6 million names were submitted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huntsville_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Huntsville-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_progress">
        Mission progress
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mission progress">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1996" data-file-width="2661" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg/220px-Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg/330px-Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg/440px-Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goddard_Spaceflight_Center_Laser_Ranging_Facility.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         In this image, the lower of the two green beams is from the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter's dedicated tracker.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On June 23, 2009, the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter entered into orbit around the Moon after a four-and-a-half-day journey from the Earth. When launched, the spacecraft was aimed at a point ahead of the Moon's position. A mid-course correction was required during the trip in order for the spacecraft to correctly enter Lunar orbit. Once the spacecraft reached the
       <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">
        far side of the Moon
       </a>
       , its rocket motor was fired in order for it to be captured by the Moon's gravity into an elliptical lunar orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A series of four rocket burns over the next four days put the satellite into its commissioning phase orbit where each
       <a href="#Onboard_instruments">
        instrument
       </a>
       was brought online and tested. On September 15, 2009, the spacecraft started its primary mission by orbiting the Moon at about 50 km (31 mi) for one year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After completing its one-year exploration phase, in September 2010, LRO was handed over to NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Science_Mission_Directorate" title="Science Mission Directorate">
        Science Mission Directorate
       </a>
       to continue the science phase of the mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It will continue in its 50 km circular orbit, but eventually will be transitioned into a fuel-conserving elliptical orbit for the remainder of the mission.
      </p>
      <p>
       NASA's LCROSS mission culminated with two lunar impacts at 11:31 and 11:36 UTC on October 9.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The goal of the impact was the search for water in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cabeus_(crater)" title="Cabeus (crater)">
        Cabeus crater
       </a>
       near the Moon's south pole,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and preliminary results indicated the presence of both water and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">
        hydroxyl
       </a>
       , an ion related to water.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LROtopography.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/LROtopography.jpg/220px-LROtopography.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/LROtopography.jpg/330px-LROtopography.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/LROtopography.jpg/440px-LROtopography.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LROtopography.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         LOLA data provides three complementary views of the near side of the Moon: the topography (left) along with maps of the surface slope values (middle) and the roughness of the topography (right). All three views are centered on the relatively young impact crater
         <a href="/wiki/Tycho_(lunar_crater)" title="Tycho (lunar crater)">
          Tycho
         </a>
         , with the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orientale_basin" title="Orientale basin">
          Orientale basin
         </a>
         on the left side.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On January 4, 2011, the
       <a href="/wiki/Mini-RF" title="Mini-RF">
        Mini-RF
       </a>
       instrument team for the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter (LRO) found that the Mini-RF radar transmitter had suffered an anomaly. Mini-RF has suspended normal operations. Despite being unable to transmit, the instrument is being used to collect
       <a href="/wiki/Bistatic_radar" title="Bistatic radar">
        bistatic radar
       </a>
       observations using radar transmissions from the Earth. The Mini-RF instrument has already met its science mission success criteria by collecting more than 400 strips of radar data since September 2010.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2013, NASA tested one-way laser communication with LRO by sending an image of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">
        Mona Lisa
       </a>
       to the Lunar Orbiter Laser Altimeter (LOLA) instrument on LRO from the Next Generation Satellite Laser Ranging (NGSLR) station at NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">
        Goddard Space Flight Center
       </a>
       in Greenbelt, Md.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In May 2015, LRO's orbit was altered to fly 20 km (12 mi) above the Moon's south pole, allowing higher resolution data to be obtained from the Lunar Orbiter Laser Altimeter (LOLA) and Diviner instruments over the permanently shadowed craters there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Results">
        Results
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Results">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       On August 21, 2009, the spacecraft, along with the
       <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">
        Chandrayaan-1
       </a>
       orbiter, attempted to perform a
       <a href="/wiki/Bistatic_radar" title="Bistatic radar">
        bistatic radar
       </a>
       experiment to detect the presence of water ice on the lunar surface,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the test was unsuccessful.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On December 17, 2010, a topographic map of the Moon based on data gathered by the LOLA instrument was released to the public.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is the most accurate topographic map of the Moon to date. It will continue to be updated as more data is acquired.
      </p>
      <p>
       On March 15, 2011, the final set of data from the exploration phase of the mission was released to the NASA
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Data_System" title="Planetary Data System">
        Planetary Data System
       </a>
       . The spacecraft's seven instruments delivered more than 192 terabytes of data. LRO has already collected as much data as all other planetary missions combined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This volume of data is possible because the Moon is so close and because LRO has its own dedicated ground station and doesn't have to share time on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">
        Deep Space Network
       </a>
       . Among the latest products is a global map with a resolution of 100 m/pixel (330 ft/pixel) from the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter Camera (LROC).
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 2015, the LROC team reported having imaged the location of an impact whose flash was observed from Earth on March 17, 2013. The team found the crater by going back to images taken in the first year or two and comparing them to images taken after the impact, called temporal pairs. The images revealed splotches, small areas whose reflectance is markedly different than that of the surrounding terrain, presumably from disruption of the surface by recent impacts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By September 2015, LROC had imaged nearly three-fourths of the lunar surface at high resolution, revealing more than 3,000
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lobate_scarp" title="Lobate scarp">
        lobate scarps
       </a>
       . Their global distribution and orientation suggests that the faults are created as the Moon shrinks, with influence by gravitational tidal forces from Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 2016, the LROC team reported the use of 14,092 NAC temporal pairs to discover over 47,000 new splotches on the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The mission maintains a full list of publications with science results on its website.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>

      <div class="thumb noresize tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:608px;">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="">
        <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg" title="Tycho crater's central peak complex casts a long, dark shadow near local sunrise.">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="9860" data-file-width="21467" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg/600px-LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg/900px-LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg/1200px-LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg 2x" width="600"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:LRO_Tycho_Central_Peak.jpg" title="File:LRO Tycho Central Peak.jpg">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Tycho_(lunar_crater)" title="Tycho (lunar crater)">
          Tycho crater's
         </a>
         central peak complex casts a long, dark shadow near local sunrise.

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=conference&amp;rft.btitle=Impact+of+Secondary+Surface+Changes+on+Regolith+Gardening&amp;rft.date=2016-03&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2016LPI....47.2645S&amp;rft.aulast=Speyerer&amp;rft.aufirst=E.+J.&amp;rft.au=Povilaitis%2C+R.+Z.&amp;rft.au=Robinson%2C+M.+S.&amp;rft.au=Thomas%2C+P.+C.&amp;rft.au=Wagner%2C+R.+V.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.hou.usra.edu%2Fmeetings%2Flpsc2016%2Fpdf%2F2645.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALunar+Reconnaissance+Orbiter">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-56">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

           </a>
